CUSTOM AUTOSHOP PLATFORM Introduction The automobile service and customization industry involves complex operations such as vehicle servicing, modification management, inventory tracking, and billing. Many small and medium workshops still rely on manual processes or basic software systems, which often lead to data inconsistency, inefficient workflow management, and lack of transparency in pricing and inventory usage. COPR (Custom Autoshop Platform) is designed as a database-driven system that integrates multiple operational modules including customer management, vehicle tracking, customization services, inventory handling, and taxation. The system aims to streamline workshop operations by maintaining structured data, improving accuracy, and enabling efficient decision-making. By leveraging a well-designed relational database, COPR ensures proper handling of multi-branch operations, supplier management, and service workflows, making it a scalable and reliable solution for modern autoshops. Motivation The motivation behind COPR comes from the challenges faced by automobile workshops in managing customization and service operations effectively. Traditional systems often lack proper structure, leading to issues such as incorrect billing, poor inventory tracking, and inefficient handling of multiple customer requests. Additionally, customization services involve complex pricing that depends on vehicle type, modification type, and service level. Without a structured database system, maintaining such dependencies becomes difficult and error-prone. COPR is motivated by the need to create a centralized, secure, and normalized database system that can handle multiple operations efficiently while ensuring data consistency and accuracy. The project aims to demonstrate how database design principles can be applied to solve real-world business problems. Scope The scope of COPR includes the design and implementation of a database system that supports: Customer and user management with role-based access Vehicle registration and tracking Customization order processing with tier-based pricing Service order management and mechanic assignment Inventory management with supplier tracking Multi-branch operations with warehouse support Tax calculation and billing breakdown The system focuses on backend database design and logic implementation. Advanced features such as mobile applications, real-time analytics, and AI-based recommendations are considered future enhancements and are outside the current scope. Problem Statement Traditional automobile workshop management systems are often manual or poorly structured, leading to inefficiencies in handling customer orders, inventory tracking, and service management. These systems fail to properly manage complex relationships such as customization pricing, multi-branch operations, and inventory dependencies. The problem is to design a structured relational database system that can efficiently manage customer data, vehicle information, customization and service orders, inventory operations, and taxation, while ensuring data integrity, scalability, and consistency. Project Requirements Hardware Requirements: Personal Computer or Laptop Minimum 4GB RAM Software Requirements: Database Management System (MySQL) SQL Interface / MySQL Command Line / Workbench Operating System (Windows / Linux) Identification of Entities and Relationships Entities User : Stores login credentials and role-based access Role : Defines different system roles such as admin, mechanic, and customer Customer : Stores customer details Vehicle : Contains vehicle information linked to customers Vehicle_Type : Defines types of vehicles (SUV, Sedan, etc.) Modification : Represents customization categories Modification_Tier : Defines levels of modification Customization_Order : Stores customer customization requests Service_Order : Stores service-related operations Mechanic : Contains mechanic details and availability Inventory : Stores parts and stock details Supplier : Stores supplier information Branch : Represents different workshop locations Warehouse : Stores inventory per branch Tax : Stores tax rules and percentages Relationships A  User  is assigned a  Role A  Customer  is linked to a  User A  Customer  owns multiple  Vehicles A  Vehicle  belongs to one  Vehicle_Type A  Customer  places  Customization_Orders A  Customization_Order  includes multiple  Modifications A  Vehicle  undergoes  Service_Orders A  Mechanic  is assigned to multiple  Service_Orders A  Supplier  supplies  Inventory A  Branch  manages  Service_Orders  and  Inventory Tax  is applied to  Customization_Orders Construction of DB Using ER Model for COPR – Custom Autoshop CHAPTER 3 COMPLEX QUERIES BASED ON CONSTRAINTS, SETS, JOINS, VIEWS, TRIGGERS AND CURSORS FOR COPR – CUSTOM AUTOSHOP PLATFORM Adding Constraints and Queries Based on Constraints Question 1:  Add a constraint to ensure inventory quantity cannot be negative. SQL Statement:  ALTER TABLE Inventory  ADD CONSTRAINT chk_quantity_positive  CHECK (quantity &gt;= 0); Output: Question 2:  Add a unique constraint to ensure no two suppliers have the same name. SQL Statement:  ALTER TABLE Supplier  ADD CONSTRAINT unique_supplier_name  UNIQUE (supplier_name); Output: Question 3:  Attempt to insert invalid inventory data to test constraint. SQL Statement:  INSERT INTO Inventory (inventory_id, warehouse_id, supplier_id, part_name, quantity, cost_price)  VALUES (99, 1, 1, 'Test Part', -5, 1000); Output: Queries Based on Aggregate Functions Question 1:  Find total number of customization orders per customer. SQL Statement:  SELECT customer_id, COUNT(*) AS total_orders  FROM Customization_Order  GROUP BY customer_id; Output: Question 2:  Find total inventory quantity per warehouse. SQL Statement:  SELECT warehouse_id, SUM(quantity) AS total_stock  FROM Inventory  GROUP BY warehouse_id; Output: Question 3:  Find average pricing for each vehicle type. SQL Statement:  SELECT vehicle_type_id, AVG(selling_price) AS avg_price  FROM Tier_Vehicle_Pricing  GROUP BY vehicle_type_id; Output: Complex Queries Based on Sets Question 1:  Retrieve combined list of supplier names and branch names. SQL Statement:  SELECT supplier_name AS name FROM Supplier  UNION  SELECT branch_name FROM Branch; Output: Question 2:  Find users who are customers and have placed orders. SQL Statement:  SELECT c.user_id  FROM Customer c  JOIN Customization_Order o ON c.customer_id = o.customer_id; Output: Question 3:  Find vehicles that exist but have no service orders. SQL Statement:  SELECT vehicle_id  FROM Vehicle  WHERE vehicle_id NOT IN  (SELECT vehicle_id FROM Service_Order); Output: Complex Queries Based on Subqueries Question 1:  Find inventory items with quantity greater than average. SQL Statement:  SELECT part_name, quantity  FROM Inventory  WHERE quantity &gt; (SELECT AVG(quantity) FROM Inventory); Output: Question 2:  Find branches with more than one service order. SQL Statement:  SELECT branch_id  FROM Service_Order  GROUP BY branch_id  HAVING COUNT(*) &gt; 1; Output: Question 3:  Find latest service order. SQL Statement:  SELECT *  FROM Service_Order  WHERE created_at = (SELECT MAX(created_at) FROM Service_Order); Output: Complex Queries Based on Joins Question 1:  Display customization orders with customer and vehicle details. SQL Statement:  SELECT o.order_id, c.customer_id, v.registration_number  FROM Customization_Order o  JOIN Customer c ON o.customer_id = c.customer_id  JOIN Vehicle v ON o.vehicle_id = v.vehicle_id; Output: Question 2:  Show all inventory items with supplier details. SQL Statement:  SELECT i.part_name, s.supplier_name, i.quantity  FROM Inventory i  JOIN Supplier s ON i.supplier_id = s.supplier_id; Output: Question 3:  Show service orders with mechanic assignment. SQL Statement:  SELECT so.service_id, m.mechanic_id, so.service_status  FROM Service_Order so  JOIN Job_Assignment ja ON so.service_id = ja.service_id  JOIN Mechanic m ON ja.mechanic_id = m.mechanic_id; Output: Complex Queries Based on Views Question 1:  Create view for total orders per customer. SQL Statement:  CREATE VIEW customer_order_summary AS  SELECT customer_id, COUNT(*) AS total_orders  FROM Customization_Order  GROUP BY customer_id; Output: Question 2:  Create inventory summary view. SQL Statement:  CREATE VIEW inventory_summary AS  SELECT warehouse_id, SUM(quantity) AS total_stock  FROM Inventory  GROUP BY warehouse_id; Output: Question 3:  Query high stock warehouses. SQL Statement:  SELECT * FROM inventory_summary  WHERE total_stock &gt; 20; Output: Complex Queries Based on Triggers Question 1:  Create trigger to prevent negative inventory. SQL Statement:  DELIMITER //  CREATE TRIGGER prevent_negative_inventory  BEFORE UPDATE ON Inventory  FOR EACH ROW  BEGIN  IF NEW.quantity &lt; 0 THEN  SIGNAL SQLSTATE '45000'  SET MESSAGE_TEXT = 'Quantity cannot be negative';  END IF;  END //  DELIMITER ; Output: Question 2:  Create audit table and trigger for orders. SQL Statement:  CREATE TABLE Order_Audit (  audit_id INT AUTO_INCREMENT PRIMARY KEY,  order_id INT,  created_at DATETIME DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP  ); DELIMITER //  CREATE TRIGGER log_order  AFTER INSERT ON Customization_Order  FOR EACH ROW  BEGIN  INSERT INTO Order_Audit(order_id) VALUES (NEW.order_id);  END //  DELIMITER ; Output: Question 3:  Test trigger functionality. SQL Statement:  INSERT INTO Customization_Order VALUES (6000,101,201,1,'PENDING',NOW());  SELECT * FROM Order_Audit; Output:  Complex Queries Based on Cursors CHAPTER 4 PROGRAM: NORMALIZATION USING EXISTING TABLES 4.1 Analyse the Pitfalls in Relations SQL Statement:  SELECT customer_id, vehicle_id, branch_id  FROM Customization_Order; Output: Explanation:  The same customer, vehicle, and branch data may appear multiple times across different orders, leading to redundancy and possible update inconsistencies. 4.2 First Normal Form (1NF) SQL Statement:  SELECT * FROM Order_Modification; Output: Explanation:  First Normal Form ensures that all attributes contain atomic values and no repeating groups. Each modification is stored as a separate row, ensuring data is indivisible. 4.3 Second Normal Form (2NF) SQL Statement:  SELECT * FROM Tier_Vehicle_Pricing; Output: Explanation:  Second Normal Form removes partial dependency. All non-key attributes depend on the full primary key, such as pricing depending on both vehicle type and modification tier. 4.4 Third Normal Form (3NF) SQL Statement:  SELECT * FROM Branch;  SELECT * FROM Supplier;  SELECT * FROM Vehicle_Type; Output: Explanation:  Third Normal Form removes transitive dependency. Attributes like branch_name and supplier_name depend only on their primary keys and not on other non-key attributes. 4.5 Boyce-Codd Normal Form (BCNF) SQL Statement:  DESCRIBE Customization_Order; Output: Explanation:  BCNF ensures that every determinant is a candidate key. All attributes in the table are fully dependent on the primary key without any anomalies. 4.6 Fourth Normal Form (4NF) SQL Statement:  SELECT * FROM Order_Modification;  SELECT * FROM Inventory_Reservation; Output: Explanation:  Fourth Normal Form eliminates multi-valued dependencies. Multiple modifications and inventory reservations are stored in separate tables to avoid duplication. 4.7 Fifth Normal Form (5NF) SQL Statement:  SELECT o.order_id, c.customer_id, v.vehicle_id  FROM Customization_Order o  JOIN Customer c ON o.customer_id = c.customer_id  JOIN Vehicle v ON o.vehicle_id = v.vehicle_id; Output: Explanation:  Fifth Normal Form ensures that data can be decomposed into smaller tables and reconstructed using joins without loss of information. CHAPTER 5 IMPLEMENTATION OF CONCURRENCY CONTROL AND RECOVERY MECHANISMS 5.1 Introduction to Transactions 5.1.1 Properties Atomicity – All operations in a transaction either complete fully or fail entirely.  Consistency – The database remains valid before and after the transaction.  Isolation – Transactions execute independently without interference.  Durability – Once committed, changes are permanently stored. 5.1.2 States Active  Partially Committed  Committed  Failed 5.2 Transaction Control Language (TCL) 5.2.1 Savepoint SQL Statement:  SAVEPOINT sp1; Output: 5.2.2 Commit SQL Statement:  COMMIT; Output: 5.2.3 Rollback SQL Statement:  ROLLBACK; Output: 5.3 Transactions 5.3.1 Transaction 1 SQL Statement:  START TRANSACTION;  INSERT INTO Customer VALUES (999, 1);  COMMIT; Output: Transaction 2 SQL Statement:  START TRANSACTION;  UPDATE Inventory  SET quantity = quantity - 2  WHERE inventory_id = 1;  COMMIT; Output: Transaction 3 SQL Statement:  START TRANSACTION;  INSERT INTO Customization_Order VALUES (7000,101,201,1,'PENDING',NOW());  ROLLBACK; Output: Transaction 4 SQL Statement:  START TRANSACTION;  UPDATE Service_Order  SET service_status = 'COMPLETED'  WHERE service_id = 1;  SAVEPOINT sp1;  UPDATE Service_Order  SET service_status = 'FAILED'  WHERE service_id = 1;  ROLLBACK TO sp1;  COMMIT; Output: Transaction 5 SQL Statement:  START TRANSACTION;  INSERT INTO Inventory_Reservation VALUES (10,1,5001,2);  COMMIT; Output: 5.3 Concurrency Control 5.3.1 Locking Commands a. Row-Level Locking SQL Statement:  START TRANSACTION;  SELECT * FROM Inventory  WHERE inventory_id = 1  FOR UPDATE;  COMMIT; Output: b. Table-Level Locking SQL Statement:  LOCK TABLES Inventory WRITE;  UNLOCK TABLES; Output: 5.3.2 Example SQL Statement:  START TRANSACTION;  UPDATE Inventory  SET quantity = quantity - 1  WHERE inventory_id = 1;  SELECT * FROM Inventory WHERE inventory_id = 1;  COMMIT; Output: <w:pgSz w:h="16840" w:w="11920" w:orient="portrait"/><w:pgMar w:bottom="280" w:top="1300" w:left="708" w:right="425"
